      <p>
       <b>
        Vulgar Latin
       </b>
       or
       <b>
        Sermo Vulgaris
       </b>
       ("common speech") was the
       <a href="/wiki/Sociolect" title="Sociolect">
        nonstandard form(s)
       </a>
       of Latin (as opposed to
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Classical_Latin" title="Classical Latin">
         classical
        </a>
       </i>
       or refined or 'proper' literary version of the language) spoken in the Mediterranean region during and after the classical period of the
       <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">
        Roman Empire
       </a>
       . It is from Vulgar Latin that the
       <a href="/wiki/Romance_languages" title="Romance languages">
        Romance languages
       </a>
       developed; the best known are the national languages
       <a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">
        Italian
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">
        Spanish
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_language" title="Portuguese language">
        Portuguese
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Romanian_language" title="Romanian language">
        Romanian
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">
        French
       </a>
       . Works written in Latin during classical times and the earlier Middle Ages used
       <a href="/wiki/Classical_Latin" title="Classical Latin">
        Classical Latin
       </a>
       rather than Vulgar Latin, with very few exceptions (most notably sections of
       <a href="/wiki/Petronius" title="Petronius">
        Gaius Petronius
       </a>
       '
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Satyricon" title="Satyricon">
         Satyricon
        </a>
       </i>
       ). Because of its nonstandard nature, Vulgar Latin had no official
       <a href="/wiki/Orthography" title="Orthography">
        orthography
       </a>
       . Vulgar Latin is sometimes also called
       <a href="/wiki/Colloquialism" title="Colloquialism">
        colloquial
       </a>
       Latin,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       or
       <b>
        Common Romance
       </b>
       (particularly in the late stage). In
       <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_Latin" title="Renaissance Latin">
        Renaissance Latin
       </a>
       , Vulgar Latin was called
       <i>
        vulgare Latinum
       </i>
       or
       <i>
        Latinum vulgare.
       </i>
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2017)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       By its nature Vulgar Latin varied greatly by region and by time period. A few major divisions can be seen, however. Vulgar Latin dialects began to significantly diverge from Classical Latin during the 3rd century during the classical period of the Roman Empire. Nevertheless through the 6th century the most widely spoken dialects were still similar to and mostly mutually intelligible with Classical Latin. Latin 'died' when it ceased to be anybody's natural mother tongue and had to be learned; "the verb system, however, seems to have remained virtually intact throughout the fifth century"..."the transformation of the language, from structures we call Latin into structures we call Romance, lasted from the third or fourth century until the eighth; "we could say Latin 'died' in the first part of the eighth century; "in Italy the first signs" that people were aware of the difference between the everyday language they spoke and the written form is in the mid-tenth century, Herman, Jozsef, Vulgar Latin, 1967, pp. 109-115. The period of most rapid change occurred from the second half of the 7th century. Until then the spoken and written form (though with many 'vulgar features) were regarded as one language, p. 112.
      </p>
      <p>
       The language called
       <i>
        Proto-Romance
       </i>
       developed during governance of Germanic rulers.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Similarly in the
       <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">
        Eastern Roman Empire
       </a>
       as Latin faded as the Court language in the course of the 5th century. The Vulgar Latin spoken in the Balkans north of Greece and southern Bulgaria became heavily influenced by Greek and Slavic and also became radically different from Classical Latin and from the proto-Romance of Western Europe.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Vulgar Latin diverged into distinct languages beginning in the 9th century
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>

      <p>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Origin_of_the_term">
        Origin of the term
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Vulgar_Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Origin of the term">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       The term "common speech" (
       <i>
        sermo vulgaris
       </i>
       ), which later became "Vulgar Latin", was used by inhabitants of the Roman Empire. Subsequently it became a technical term from Latin and
       <a href="/wiki/Romance_languages" title="Romance languages">
        Romance-language
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Philology" title="Philology">
        philology
       </a>
       referring to the unwritten varieties of a Latinised language spoken mainly by
       <a href="/wiki/Italo-Celtic" title="Italo-Celtic">
        Italo-Celtic
       </a>
       populations governed by the
       <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">
        Roman Republic
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">
        Roman Empire
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Traces of their language appear in some inscriptions, such as graffiti or advertisements. The educated population mainly responsible for
       <a href="/wiki/Classical_Latin" title="Classical Latin">
        Classical Latin
       </a>
       may also have spoken Vulgar Latin in certain contexts depending on their socioeconomic background. The term was first used improperly in that sense by the pioneers of Romance-language philology:
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Juste_Marie_Raynouard" title="François Juste Marie Raynouard">
        François Juste Marie Raynouard
       </a>
       (1761–1836) and
       <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Christian_Diez" title="Friedrich Christian Diez">
        Friedrich Christian Diez
       </a>
       (1794–1876).
      </p>
      <p>
       In the course of his studies on the lyrics of songs written by the
       <a href="/wiki/Troubadour" title="Troubadour">
        troubadours
       </a>
       of
       <a href="/wiki/Provence" title="Provence">
        Provence
       </a>
       , which had already been studied by
       <a href="/wiki/Dante_Alighieri" title="Dante Alighieri">
        Dante Alighieri
       </a>
       and published in
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/De_vulgari_eloquentia" title="De vulgari eloquentia">
         De vulgari eloquentia
        </a>
       </i>
       , Raynouard noticed that the Romance languages derived in part from lexical, morphological, and syntactic features that were Latin, but were not preferred in
       <a href="/wiki/Classical_Latin" title="Classical Latin">
        Classical Latin
       </a>
       . He hypothesized an intermediate phase and identified it with the
       <i>
        Romana lingua
       </i>
       , a term that in countries speaking Romance languages meant "nothing more or less than the vulgar speech as opposed to literary or grammatical Latin."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Diez, the principal founder of Romance-language philology, impressed by the
       <a href="/wiki/Comparative_method_(linguistics)" title="Comparative method (linguistics)">
        comparative methods
       </a>
       of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jakob_Grimm" title="Jakob Grimm">
        Jakob Grimm
       </a>
       in
       <i>
        Deutsche Grammatik
       </i>
       , which came out in 1819 and was the first to use such methods in philology, decided to apply them to the Romance languages and discovered Raynouard's work,
       <i>
        Grammaire comparée des langues de l'Europe latine dans leurs rapports avec la langue des troubadours
       </i>
       , published in 1821. Describing himself as a pupil of Raynouard, he went on to expand the concept to all Romance languages, not just the speech of the troubadours, on a systematic basis, thereby becoming the originator of a new field of scholarly inquiry.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Diez, in his signal work on the topic,
       <i>
        Grammatik der romanischen Sprachen
       </i>
       , "Grammar of the Romance Languages," first published in 1836–1843 and multiple times thereafter, after enumerating six Romance languages that he compared: Italian and Wallachian (
       <i>
        i.e.
       </i>
       , Romanian) (east); Spanish and Portuguese (southwest); and Provençal and French (northwest), asserts that they had their origin in Latin, but
       <i>
        nicht aus dem classischen Latein
       </i>
       , "not from classical Latin," rather
       <i>
        aus der römischen Volkssprache oder Volksmundart
       </i>
       , "from the Roman popular language or popular dialect".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-7">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       These terms, as he points out later in the work, are a translation into German of Dante's
       <i>
        vulgare latinum
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        Latinum vulgare
       </i>
       , and the Italian of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boccaccio" title="Boccaccio">
        Boccaccio
       </a>
       ,
       <i>
        latino volgare
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       These names in turn are at the end of a tradition extending to the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_republic" title="Roman republic">
        Roman republic
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The concepts and vocabulary from which
       <i>
        vulgare latinum
       </i>
       descend were known in the classical period and are to be found amply represented in the unabridged Latin dictionary, starting in the late Roman republic.
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marcus_Tullius_Cicero" title="Marcus Tullius Cicero">
        Marcus Tullius Cicero
       </a>
       , a prolific writer, whose works have survived in large quantity, and who serves as a standard of Latin, and his contemporaries in addition to recognizing the
       <i>
        lingua Latina
       </i>
       also knew varieties of "speech" under the name
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/sermo#Latin" title="wikt:sermo">
         sermo
        </a>
       </i>
       . Latin could be
       <i>
        sermo Latinus
       </i>
       , but in addition was a variety known as
       <i>
        sermo vulgaris
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        sermo vulgi
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        sermo plebeius
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        sermo quotidianus
       </i>
       . These modifiers inform post-classical readers that a conversational Latin existed, which was used by the masses (
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/vulgus#Latin" title="wikt:vulgus">
         vulgus
        </a>
       </i>
       ) in daily speaking (
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/quotidianus#Latin" title="wikt:quotidianus">
         quotidianus
        </a>
       </i>
       ) and was perceived as lower-class (
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/plebeius#Latin" title="wikt:plebeius">
         plebeius
        </a>
       </i>
       ).
      </p>
      <p>
       These vocabulary items manifest no opposition to the written language. There was an opposition to higher-class, or family Latin (good family) in
       <i>
        sermo familiaris
       </i>
       and very rarely literature might be termed
       <i>
        sermo nobilis
       </i>
       . The supposed "sermo classicus" is a scholarly fiction unattested in the dictionary. All kinds of
       <i>
        sermo
       </i>
       were spoken only, not written. If one wanted to refer to what in post-classical times was called classical Latin one resorted to the concept of
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/latinitas#Latin" title="wikt:latinitas">
         latinitas
        </a>
       </i>
       ("latinity") or
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/latine#Latin" title="wikt:latine">
         latine
        </a>
       </i>
       (adverb).
      </p>
      <p>
       If one spoke in the
       <i>
        lingua
       </i>
       or
       <i>
        sermo Latinus
       </i>
       one merely spoke Latin, but if one spoke
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/latine#Latin" title="wikt:latine">
         latine
        </a>
       </i>
       or
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/latinius#Latin" title="wikt:latinius">
         latinius
        </a>
       </i>
       ("more Latinish") one spoke good Latin, and formal Latin had
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Latinitas#Latin" title="wikt:Latinitas">
         latinitas
        </a>
       </i>
       , the quality of good Latin, about it. After the fall of the empire and the transformation of spoken Latin into the early Romance languages the only representative of the Latin language was written Latin, which became known as
       <i>
        classicus
       </i>
       , "classy" Latin. The original opposition was between formal or implied good Latin and informal or Vulgar Latin. The spoken/written dichotomy is entirely philological.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Sources">
        Sources
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Vulgar_Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Sources">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Vulgar Latin is a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blanket_term" title="Blanket term">
        blanket term
       </a>
       covering the popular
       <a href="/wiki/Dialect" title="Dialect">
        dialects
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Sociolect" title="Sociolect">
        sociolects
       </a>
       of the
       <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">
        Latin language
       </a>
       throughout its range, from the hypothetical
       <i>
        prisca latinitas
       </i>
       of unknown or poorly remembered times in early Latium, to the language spoken around the fall of the empire. Although making it clear that
       <i>
        sermo vulgaris
       </i>
       existed, ancient writers said very little about it. Because it was not
       <a href="/wiki/Transcription_(linguistics)" title="Transcription (linguistics)">
        transcribed
       </a>
       , it can only be studied indirectly. Knowledge comes from these chief sources:
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
        <a href="#cite_note-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <ol>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Solecism" title="Solecism">
         Solecisms
        </a>
        , especially in
        <a href="/wiki/Late_Latin" title="Late Latin">
         Late Latin
        </a>
        texts.
       </li>
       <li>
        Mention of it by ancient grammarians, including
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prescriptive_grammar" title="Prescriptive grammar">
         prescriptive grammar
        </a>
        texts from the Late Latin period condemning linguistic "errors" that represent spoken Latin.
       </li>
       <li>
        The
        <a href="/wiki/Comparative_method_(linguistics)" title="Comparative method (linguistics)">
         comparative method
        </a>
        , which reconstructs Proto-Romance, a hypothetical vernacular
        <a href="/wiki/Proto-language" title="Proto-language">
         proto-language
        </a>
        from which the Romance languages descended.
       </li>
       <li>
        Some literary works written in a lower
        <a href="/wiki/Register_(sociolinguistics)" title="Register (sociolinguistics)">
         register
        </a>
        of Latin provide a glimpse into the world of Vulgar Latin in the classical period: the dialogues of the plays of
        <a href="/wiki/Plautus" title="Plautus">
         Plautus
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/Terence" title="Terence">
         Terence
        </a>
        , being comedies with many characters who were slaves, and the speech of freedmen in the
        <i>
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cena_Trimalchionis" title="Cena Trimalchionis">
          Cena Trimalchionis
         </a>
        </i>
        by
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Petronius_Arbiter" title="Petronius Arbiter">
         Petronius Arbiter
        </a>
        .
       </li>
      </ol>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="History">
        History
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Vulgar_Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: History">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sacramenta_Argentariae_(pars_brevis).jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="357" data-file-width="422" height="254" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Sacramenta_Argentariae_%28pars_brevis%29.jpg/300px-Sacramenta_Argentariae_%28pars_brevis%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/29/Sacramenta_Argentariae_%28pars_brevis%29.jpg 1.5x" width="300"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sacramenta_Argentariae_(pars_brevis).jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         An extract of the
         <a href="/wiki/Oaths_of_Strasbourg" title="Oaths of Strasbourg">
          Oaths of Strasbourg
         </a>
         , the earliest French text.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The original written Latin language (what is today referred to as
       <i>
        Classical Latin
       </i>
       ) was adapted from the actual spoken language of the Latins, with some minor modifications, long before the rise of the
       <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">
        Roman Empire
       </a>
       . As with many languages, over time the spoken
       <i>
        vulgar
       </i>
       language diverged from the written language with the written language remaining somewhat static. During the classical period spoken (Vulgar) Latin still remained largely common across the Empire, some minor dialectal differences notwithstanding.
      </p>
      <p>
       The collapse of the
       <a href="/wiki/Western_Roman_Empire" title="Western Roman Empire">
        Western Roman Empire
       </a>
       rapidly began to change this. The former western provinces became increasingly isolated from the
       <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">
        Eastern Roman Empire
       </a>
       , leading to a rapid divergence in the Latin spoken on either side. In the West an even more complex transformation was occurring. A blending of cultures was occurring between the former Roman citizens who were fluent in the
       <i>
        proper
       </i>
       Latin speech (which was already substantially different from Classical Latin), and the new
       <a href="/wiki/Goths" title="Goths">
        Gothic
       </a>
       rulers who, though largely Latinised, tended to speak Latin poorly, speaking what could be considered a pidgin of Latin and their Germanic mother tongue, though this changed over time.
      </p>
      <p>
       The vulgar Latin language that continued to evolve after the establishment of the successor kingdoms of the Roman State incorporated Germanic vocabulary but with minimal influences from Germanic grammar (Germanic languages did not displace Latin except in northern Belgium, the Rhineland Moselle region and north of the Alps). For a few centuries this language remained relatively common across most of Western Europe (hence the fact that Italian, Spanish, French, etc. are far more similar to each other than to Classical Latin), though regional dialects were already developing. As early as 722, in a face to face meeting between
       <a href="/wiki/Pope_Gregory_II" title="Pope Gregory II">
        Pope Gregory II
       </a>
       , born and raised in Rome, and
       <a href="/wiki/Saint_Boniface" title="Saint Boniface">
        Saint Boniface
       </a>
       , an
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon" title="Anglo-Saxon">
        Anglo-Saxon
       </a>
       , Boniface complained that he found Pope Gregory's Latin speech difficult to understand, a clear sign of the transformation of Vulgar Latin in two regions of western Europe.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
        <a href="#cite_note-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Soon Classical Latin and Vulgar Latin came to be viewed as distinct languages. At the third
       <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Tours" title="Council of Tours">
        Council of Tours
       </a>
       in 813,
       <a href="/wiki/Priest" title="Priest">
        priests
       </a>
       were ordered to preach in the vernacular language – either in the
       <i>
        rustica lingua romanica
       </i>
       (Vulgar Latin), or in the
       <a href="/wiki/West_Germanic_languages" title="West Germanic languages">
        Germanic vernaculars
       </a>
       – since the common people could no longer understand formal Latin. Within a generation, the
       <a href="/wiki/Oaths_of_Strasbourg" title="Oaths of Strasbourg">
        Oaths of Strasbourg
       </a>
       (842), a treaty between
       <a href="/wiki/Charlemagne" title="Charlemagne">
        Charlemagne
       </a>
       's grandsons
       <a href="/wiki/Charles_the_Bald" title="Charles the Bald">
        Charles the Bald
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Louis_the_German" title="Louis the German">
        Louis the German
       </a>
       , was proffered and recorded in a language that was already distinct from Latin. József Herman states:
      </p>
      <blockquote class="templatequote">
       <p>
        It seems certain that in the sixth century, and quite likely into the early parts of the seventh century, people in the main Romanized areas could still largely understand the biblical and liturgical texts and the commentaries (of greater or lesser simplicity) that formed part of the rites and of religious practice, and that even later, throughout the seventh century, saints' lives written in Latin could be read aloud to the congregations with an expectation that they would be understood. We can also deduce however, that in Gaul, from the central part of the eighth century onwards, many people, including several of the clerics, were not able to understand even the most straightforward religious texts.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Herman_11-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-Herman-11">
          [11]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </p>
      </blockquote>
      <p>
       By the end of the first millennium, dialects had diverged so far that some of the more geographically distant ones had become mutually unintelligible and distinct. With the evolved Latin vernaculars viewed as different languages with local norms, specific
       <a href="/wiki/Orthography" title="Orthography">
        orthographies
       </a>
       would in time be developed for some. Since all modern Romance varieties are continuations of this evolution, Vulgar Latin is not extinct but survives in variously evolved forms as today's Romance languages and dialects. In Romance-speaking Europe, recognition of the common origin of Romance varieties was replaced by labels recognizing and implicitly accentuating local differences in linguistic features. Some Romance languages evolved more than others. In terms of phonological structures, for example, a clear hierarchy from conservative to innovative is found in comparing Italian, Spanish and French (e.g. Latin
       <i>
        amica
       </i>
       &gt; Italian
       <i>
        amica
       </i>
       , Spanish
       <i>
        amiga
       </i>
       , French
       <i>
        amie
       </i>
       ; Latin
       <i>
        caput
       </i>
       &gt; Italian
       <i>
        capo
       </i>
       , Spanish
       <i>
        cabo
       </i>
       , French
       <i>
        chef
       </i>
       ).
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Oaths_of_Strasbourg" title="Oaths of Strasbourg">
        Oaths of Strasbourg
       </a>
       offer indications of the state of Gallo-Romance toward the middle of the 9th century. While the language cannot be said with any degree of certainty to be Old French in the sense of the linear precursor to today's standard French, the abundance of Gallo-Romance features provides a glimpse of some particulars of Vulgar Latin's evolution on French soil.
      </p>
      <table align="right" class="wikitable">
       <caption>
        Extract of the Romance part of the Oaths of Strasbourg (842)
       </caption>
       <tr>
        <th>
         Gallo-Romance, AD 842
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
          <a href="#cite_note-12">
           [12]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </th>
        <th>
         Vulgar Latin of Paris, circa 5th c. AD, for comparison
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
          <a href="#cite_note-13">
           [13]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </th>
        <th>
         English Translation
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         "Pro Deo amur et pro christian poblo et nostro commun salvament, d'ist di in avant, in quant Deus savir et podir me dunat, si salvarai eo cist meon fradre Karlo, et in ayudha et in cadhuna cosa si cum om per dreit son fradra salvar dift, in o quid il mi altresi fazet. Et ab Ludher nul plaid nunquam prindrai qui meon vol cist meon fradre Karlo in damno sit."
        </td>
        <td>
         "Por Deo amore et por chrestyano pob(o)lo et nostro comune salvamento de esto die en avante en quanto Deos sabere et podere me donat, sic salvarayo eo eccesto meon fradre Karlo, et en ayuda et en caduna causa, sic quomo omo per drecto son fradre salvare devet, en o qued illi me altrosic fatsyat, et ab Ludero nullo plag(i)do nonqua prendrayo, qui meon volo eccesto meon fradre Karlo en damno seat."
        </td>
        <td>
         "For the love of God and for Christendom and our common salvation, from this day onwards, as God will give me the wisdom and power, I shall protect this brother of mine Charles, with aid or anything else, as one ought to protect one's brother, so that he may do the same for me, and I shall never knowingly make any covenant with Lothair that would harm this brother of mine Charles."
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <div style="clear:both;">
      </div>

       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">

        </a>

         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <table class="plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation">
       <tr>
        <td class="mbox-image">

      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">

       <a href="/wiki/Vulgar_Latin_vocabulary" title="Vulgar Latin vocabulary">

       </a>
      </div>

      <p>
       Vulgar Latin featured a large vocabulary of words that were productive in Romance.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Phonology">
        Phonology
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Vulgar_Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Phonology">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Latin_spelling_and_pronunciation" title="Latin spelling and pronunciation">
        Latin spelling and pronunciation
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Latin_regional_pronunciation" title="Latin regional pronunciation">
        Latin regional pronunciation
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Romance_languages" title="Romance languages">
        Romance languages
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       There was no single pronunciation of Vulgar Latin, and the pronunciation of Vulgar Latin in the various Latin-speaking areas is indistinguishable from the earlier history of the phonology of the Romance languages. See the article on
       <a href="/wiki/Romance_languages" title="Romance languages">
        Romance languages
       </a>
       for more information.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Evidence_of_changes">
        Evidence of changes
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Vulgar_Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Evidence of changes">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <table class="mbox-small plainlinks sistersitebox" role="presentation" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000">
       <tr>
        <td class="mbox-image">
         <img alt="" class="noviewer" data-file-height="430" data-file-width="410" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/38px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/57px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/76px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png 2x" width="38"/>
        </td>
        <td class="mbox-text plainlist">
         Latin
         <a href="/wiki/Wikisource" title="Wikisource">
          Wikisource
         </a>
         has original text related to this article:
         <div style="margin-left: 10px;">
          <b>
           <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/la:Appendix_Probi" title="s:la:Appendix Probi">
            Appendix Probi
           </a>
          </b>
         </div>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       Evidence of phonological changes can be seen in the late 3rd-century
       <a href="/wiki/Appendix_Probi" title="Appendix Probi">
        Appendix Probi
       </a>
       , a collection of glosses
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prescription_and_description" title="Prescription and description">
        prescribing
       </a>
       correct classical Latin forms for certain vulgar forms. These glosses describe:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        a process of
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Syncope_(phonetics)" title="Syncope (phonetics)">
         syncope
        </a>
        , the loss of
        <a href="/wiki/Stress_(linguistics)" title="Stress (linguistics)">
         unstressed
        </a>
        vowels in medial syllables ("
        <i>
         calida non calda
        </i>
        ");
       </li>
       <li>
        the merger of
        <a href="/wiki/Stress_(linguistics)" title="Stress (linguistics)">
         unstressed
        </a>
        pre-vocalic
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /e/
        </span>
        and short
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /i/
        </span>
        , probably as
        <a href="/wiki/Palatal_approximant" title="Palatal approximant">
         yod
        </a>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /j/
        </span>
        ("
        <i>
         vinea non vinia
        </i>
        ");
       </li>
       <li>
        the levelling of the distinction between
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /o/
        </span>
        and
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /u/
        </span>
        ("
        <i>
         coluber non colober
        </i>
        ") and
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /e/
        </span>
        and
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /i/
        </span>
        ("
        <i>
         dimidius non demedius
        </i>
        ");
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Regularization_(linguistics)" title="Regularization (linguistics)">
         regularization
        </a>
        of irregular forms ("
        <i>
         glis non glirus
        </i>
        ");
       </li>
       <li>
        regularization and emphasis of gendered forms ("
        <i>
         pauper mulier non paupera mulier
        </i>
        ");
       </li>
       <li>
        levelling of the distinction between
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /b/
        </span>
        and
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /w/
        </span>
        between vowels ("
        <i>
         bravium non brabium
        </i>
        ");
       </li>
       <li>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assimilation_(linguistics)" title="Assimilation (linguistics)">
         assimilation
        </a>
        of
        <a href="/wiki/Stop_consonant" title="Stop consonant">
         plosive
        </a>
        <a href="/wiki/Consonant_cluster" title="Consonant cluster">
         consonant clusters
        </a>
        ("
        <i>
         amycdala non amiddula"
        </i>
        );
       </li>
       <li>
        the substitution of
        <a href="/wiki/Diminutive" title="Diminutive">
         diminutives
        </a>
        for
        <a href="/wiki/Markedness" title="Markedness">
         unmarked
        </a>
        words ("
        <i>
         auris non oricla, neptis non nepticla
        </i>
        ");
       </li>
       <li>
        the loss of syllable-final nasals before /s/ ("
        <i>
         mensa non mesa
        </i>
        ") or their inappropriate insertion as a form of
        <a href="/wiki/Hypercorrection" title="Hypercorrection">
         hypercorrection
        </a>
        ("
        <i>
         formosus non formunsus
        </i>
        ");
       </li>
       <li>
        the loss of
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /h/
        </span>
        , both initially ("
        <i>
         hostiae non ostiae
        </i>
        ") and within the word ("
        <i>
         adhuc non aduc
        </i>
        ");
       </li>
       <li>
        simplification of
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /kʷ/
        </span>
        ("
        <i>
         coqui non coci
        </i>
        ").
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       Many of the forms castigated in the
       <i>
        Appendix Probi
       </i>
       proved to be the forms accepted in Romance; e.g.,
       <i>
        oricla
       </i>
       (evolved from the Classical Latin marked diminutive
       <i>
        auricula
       </i>
       ) is the source of French
       <i>
        oreille
       </i>
       , Catalan
       <i>
        orella
       </i>
       , Spanish
       <i>
        oreja
       </i>
       , Italian
       <i>
        orecchia
       </i>
       , Romanian
       <i>
        ureche
       </i>
       , Portuguese
       <i>
        orelha
       </i>
       , Sardinian
       <i>
        origra
       </i>
       'ear', not the prescribed
       <i>
        auris
       </i>
       . Development of yod from the post-nasal unstressed /e/ of
       <i>
        vinea
       </i>
       enabled the
       <a href="/wiki/Palatalization_(sound_change)" title="Palatalization (sound change)">
        palatalization
       </a>
       of /n/ that would produce French
       <i>
        vigne
       </i>
       , Italian
       <i>
        vigna
       </i>
       , Spanish
       <i>
        viña
       </i>
       , Portuguese
       <i>
        vinha
       </i>
       , Catalan
       <i>
        vinya
       </i>
       , Occitan
       <i>
        vinha
       </i>
       , Friulan
       <i>
        vigne
       </i>
       , etc., 'vineyard'.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Consonant_development">
        Consonant development
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Vulgar_Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Consonant development">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Romance_languages#Consonants" title="Romance languages">
        Romance languages § Consonants
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The most significant consonant changes affecting Vulgar Latin were
       <a href="/wiki/Palatalization_(sound_change)" title="Palatalization (sound change)">
        palatalization
       </a>
       (except in
       <a href="/wiki/Sardinia" title="Sardinia">
        Sardinia
       </a>
       );
       <a href="/wiki/Lenition" title="Lenition">
        lenition
       </a>
       , including simplification of geminate consonants (in areas north and west of the
       <a href="/wiki/La_Spezia%E2%80%93Rimini_Line" title="La Spezia–Rimini Line">
        La Spezia–Rimini Line
       </a>
       , e.g. Spanish
       <i>
        digo
       </i>
       vs. Italian
       <i>
        dico
       </i>
       'I say', Spanish
       <i>
        boca
       </i>
       vs. Italian
       <i>
        bocca
       </i>
       'mouth')); and loss of final consonants.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Loss_of_final_consonants">
        Loss of final consonants
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Vulgar_Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Loss of final consonants">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       The loss of final consonants was already under way by the 1st century AD in some areas. A
       <a href="/wiki/Graffiti" title="Graffiti">
        graffito
       </a>
       at
       <a href="/wiki/Pompeii" title="Pompeii">
        Pompeii
       </a>
       reads
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        quisque ama valia
       </span>
       , which in Classical Latin would read
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        quisquis amat valeat
       </span>
       ("may whoever loves be strong/do well").
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harrington_14-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Harrington-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       (The change from
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        valeat
       </span>
       to
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        valia
       </span>
       is also an early indicator of the development of
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /j/
       </span>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Palatal_approximant" title="Palatal approximant">
        yod
       </a>
       ), which played such an important part in the development of
       <a href="/wiki/Palatalization_(sound_change)" title="Palatalization (sound change)">
        palatalization
       </a>
       .) On the other hand, this loss of final
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /t/
       </span>
       was not general.
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_Spanish" title="Old Spanish">
        Old Spanish
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Old_French" title="Old French">
        Old French
       </a>
       preserved a reflex of final
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /t/
       </span>
       up through 1100 AD or so, and modern French still maintains final
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /t/
       </span>
       in some
       <a href="/wiki/Liaison_(French)" title="Liaison (French)">
        liaison
       </a>
       environments.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Lenition_of_stops">
        Lenition of stops
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Vulgar_Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Lenition of stops">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       Areas north and west of the
       <a href="/wiki/La_Spezia%E2%80%93Rimini_Line" title="La Spezia–Rimini Line">
        La Spezia–Rimini Line
       </a>
       lenited intervocalic
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /p, t, k/
       </span>
       to
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /b, d, ɡ/
       </span>
       . This phenomenon is occasionally attested during the imperial period, but it became frequent by the 7th century. For example, in Merovingian documents,
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        rotatico
       </span>
       &gt;
       <i>
        rodatico
       </i>
       ("wheel tax").
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerman200047_15-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerman200047-15">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Simplification_of_geminates">
        Simplification of geminates
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Vulgar_Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Simplification of geminates">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       Reduction of bisyllabic clusters of identical consonants to a single syllable-initial consonant also typifies Romance north and west of La Spezia-Rimini. The results in Italian and Spanish provide clear illustrations:
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        siccus
       </span>
       &gt; Italian
       <i>
        secco
       </i>
       , Spanish
       <i>
        seco
       </i>
       ;
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        cippus
       </span>
       &gt; Italian
       <i>
        ceppo
       </i>
       , Spanish
       <i>
        cepo
       </i>
       ;
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        mittere
       </span>
       &gt; Italian
       <i>
        mettere
       </i>
       , Spanish
       <i>
        meter
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Loss_of_word-final_m">
        Loss of word-final m
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Vulgar_Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Loss of word-final m">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       The loss of the final
       <i>
        m
       </i>
       was a process which seems to have begun by the time of the earliest monuments of the Latin language. The
       <a href="/wiki/Epitaph" title="Epitaph">
        epitaph
       </a>
       of
       <a href="/wiki/Lucius_Cornelius_Scipio_Barbatus" title="Lucius Cornelius Scipio Barbatus">
        Lucius Cornelius Scipio Barbatus
       </a>
       , who died around 150 BC, reads
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        taurasia cisauna samnio cepit
       </span>
       , which in Classical Latin would be
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        taurāsiam, cisaunam, samnium cēpit
       </span>
       ("He captured Taurasia, Cisauna, and Samnium"). This however can be explained in a different way, that the inscription simply fails to note the nasality of the final vowels (like in the established custom of writing
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        cos.
       </span>
       for
       <i>
        consul
       </i>
       ).
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span id="Neutralization_of_.2Fb.2F_and_.2Fw.2F">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Neutralization_of_/b/_and_/w/">
        Neutralization of /b/ and /w/
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Vulgar_Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Neutralization of /b/ and /w/">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       Confusions between
       <i>
        b
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        v
       </i>
       show that the Classical semivowel
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /w/
       </span>
       , and intervocalic
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /b/
       </span>
       partially merged to become a
       <a href="/wiki/Voiced_bilabial_fricative" title="Voiced bilabial fricative">
        bilabial fricative
       </a>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /β/
       </span>
       (Classical semivowel
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /w/
       </span>
       became
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /β/
       </span>
       in Vulgar Latin, while
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [β]
       </span>
       became an allophone of
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /b/
       </span>
       in intervocalic position). Already by the 1st century AD, a document by one Eunus writes
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        iobe
       </span>
       for
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        iovem
       </span>
       and
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        dibi
       </span>
       for
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        divi
       </span>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
        <a href="#cite_note-16">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In most of the Romance varieties, this sound would further develop into
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /v/
       </span>
       , with the notable exception of the
       <a href="/wiki/Betacism" title="Betacism">
        betacist
       </a>
       varieties of
       <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Spanish_language#Merger_of_/b/_and_/v/" title="History of the Spanish language">
        Hispano-Romance
       </a>
       :
       <i>
        b
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        v
       </i>
       represent the same phoneme /b/ (with allophone [β]) in Modern Spanish, as well as in
       <a href="/wiki/Galician_language" title="Galician language">
        Galician
       </a>
       , northern
       <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_language" title="Portuguese language">
        Portuguese
       </a>
       and the northern dialects of
       <a href="/wiki/Catalan_language" title="Catalan language">
        Catalan
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Consonant_cluster_simplification">
        Consonant cluster simplification
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Vulgar_Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Consonant cluster simplification">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       In general, many clusters were simplified in Vulgar Latin. For example,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /ns/
       </span>
       reduced to
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /s/
       </span>
       , reflecting the fact that
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /n/
       </span>
       was no longer phonetically consonantal. In some inscriptions,
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        mensis
       </span>
       &gt;
       <i>
        mesis
       </i>
       ("month"), or
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        consul
       </span>
       &gt;
       <i>
        cosul
       </i>
       ("consul").
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerman200047_15-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerman200047-15">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Descendants of
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        mensis
       </span>
       include Portuguese
       <i>
        mês
       </i>
       , Spanish and Catalan
       <i>
        mes
       </i>
       , Old French
       <i>
        meis
       </i>
       (Modern French
       <i>
        mois
       </i>
       ), Italian
       <i>
        mese
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerman200047_15-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerman200047-15">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In some areas (including much of Italy), the clusters
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [mn]
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [kt]
       </span>
       ⟨ct⟩,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ks]
       </span>
       ⟨x⟩ were assimilated to the second element:
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [nn]
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [tt]
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ss]
       </span>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerman200047_15-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerman200047-15">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Thus, some inscriptions have
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        omnibus
       </span>
       &gt;
       <i>
        onibus
       </i>
       ("all [dative plural]"),
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        indictione
       </span>
       &gt;
       <i>
        inditione
       </i>
       ("indiction"),
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        vixit
       </span>
       &gt;
       <i>
        bissit
       </i>
       ("lived").
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerman200047_15-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerman200047-15">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Also, three-consonant clusters usually lost the middle element. For example:
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        emptores
       </span>
       &gt;
       <i>
        imtores
       </i>
       ("buyers")
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerman200047_15-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerman200047-15">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Not all areas show the same development of these clusters, however. In the East, Italian has
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [kt]
       </span>
       &gt;
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [tt]
       </span>
       , as in
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        octo
       </span>
       &gt;
       <i>
        otto
       </i>
       ("eight") or
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        nocte
       </span>
       &gt;
       <i>
        notte
       </i>
       ("night"); while Romanian has
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [kt]
       </span>
       &gt;
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [pt]
       </span>
       (
       <i>
        opt
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        noapte
       </i>
       ).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerman200047_15-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerman200047-15">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
       By contrast, in the West, the
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [k]
       </span>
       was turned into
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [j]
       </span>
       . In French and Portuguese, this caused the diphthongization of the previous vowel (
       <i>
        huit
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        oito
       </i>
       ;
       <i>
        nuit
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        noite
       </i>
       ), while in Spanish, the
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [t]
       </span>
       was
       <a href="/wiki/Palatalization_(sound_change)" title="Palatalization (sound change)">
        palatalized
       </a>
       and became
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [tʃ]
       </span>
       (
       <i>
        *oito
       </i>
       &gt;
       <i>
        ocho
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        *noite
       </i>
       &gt;
       <i>
        noche
       </i>
       )
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerman200048_17-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerman200048-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Also, many clusters including
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [j]
       </span>
       were simplified. Several of these groups seem to have never been fully stable
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify">
          <span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (October 2014)">
           clarification needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       (e.g.
       <i>
        facunt
       </i>
       for
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        faciunt
       </span>
       ). This dropping has resulted in the word
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        parietem
       </span>
       ("wall") turning into: Italian
       <i>
        parete
       </i>
       , Romanian
       <i>
        părete&gt;perete
       </i>
       , Portuguese
       <i>
        parede
       </i>
       , Spanish
       <i>
        pared
       </i>
       , or French
       <i>
        paroi
       </i>
       (Old French
       <i>
        pareid
       </i>
       ).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerman200048_17-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerman200048-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The cluster
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [kw]
       </span>
       ⟨qu⟩ was simplified to
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [k]
       </span>
       in most instances. In 435, one can find the hypercorrective spelling
       <i>
        quisquentis
       </i>
       for
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        quiescentis
       </span>
       ("of the person who rests here"). Modern languages have followed this trend, for example Latin
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        qui
       </span>
       ("who") has become Italian
       <i>
        chi
       </i>
       and French
       <i>
        qui
       </i>
       (both
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /ki/
       </span>
       ); while
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        quem
       </span>
       ("who") became
       <i>
        quien
       </i>
       (
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /kjen/
       </span>
       ) in Spanish and
       <i>
        quem
       </i>
       (
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /kẽj/
       </span>
       ) in Portuguese.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerman200048_17-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerman200048-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [kw]
       </span>
       has survived in front of
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [a]
       </span>
       in most areas, although not in French; hence Latin
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        quattuor
       </span>
       yields Spanish
       <i>
        cuatro
       </i>
       (
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /kwatro/
       </span>
       ), Portuguese
       <i>
        quatro
       </i>
       (
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /kwatru/
       </span>
       ), and Italian
       <i>
        quattro
       </i>
       (
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /kwattro/
       </span>
       ), but French
       <i>
        quatre
       </i>
       (
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /katʀ/
       </span>
       ), where the
       <i>
        qu-
       </i>
       spelling is purely etymological.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerman200048_17-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerman200048-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In Spanish, most words with consonant clusters in syllable-final position are loanwords from Classical Latin, examples are:
       <i>
        tra
        <b>
         ns
        </b>
        porte
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [tɾansˈpor.te]
       </span>
       ,
       <i>
        tra
        <b>
         ns
        </b>
        mitir
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [tɾanz.miˈtir]
       </span>
       ,
       <i>
        i
        <b>
         ns
        </b>
        talar
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ins.taˈlar]
       </span>
       ,
       <i>
        co
        <b>
         ns
        </b>
        tante
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [konsˈtante]
       </span>
       ,
       <i>
        o
        <b>
         bs
        </b>
        tante
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [oβsˈtante]
       </span>
       ,
       <i>
        o
        <b>
         bs
        </b>
        truir
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [oβsˈtɾwir]
       </span>
       ,
       <i>
        pe
        <b>
         rs
        </b>
        pectiva
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [pers.pekˈti.βa]
       </span>
       ,
       <i>
        i
        <b>
         st
        </b>
        mo
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ˈist.mo]
       </span>
       . A syllable-final position cannot be more than one consonant (one of n, r, l, s or z) in most (or all) dialects in colloquial speech, reflecting Vulgar Latin background. Realizations like
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [trasˈpor.te]
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [tɾaz.miˈtir]
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [is.taˈlar]
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [kosˈtante]
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [osˈtante]
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [osˈtɾwir]
       </span>
       , and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ˈiz.mo]
       </span>
       are very common, and in many cases, they are considered acceptable even in formal speech.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Vowel_development">
        Vowel development
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Vulgar_Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Vowel development">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Romance_languages#Vowels" title="Romance languages">
        Romance languages § Vowels
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       In general, the ten-vowel system of Classical Latin, which relied on phonemic vowel length, was newly modelled into one in which
       <a href="/wiki/Vowel_length" title="Vowel length">
        vowel length
       </a>
       distinctions lost
       <a href="/wiki/Phoneme" title="Phoneme">
        phonemic
       </a>
       importance, and qualitative distinctions of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vowel_height" title="Vowel height">
        height
       </a>
       became more prominent.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="System_in_Classical_Latin">
        System in Classical Latin
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Vulgar_Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: System in Classical Latin">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       Classical Latin had 10 different vowel
       <a href="/wiki/Phoneme" title="Phoneme">
        phonemes
       </a>
       , grouped into five pairs of short-long, ⟨
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        ă – ā, ĕ – ē, ĭ – ī, ŏ – ō, ŭ – ū
       </span>
       ⟩. It also had four
       <a href="/wiki/Diphthong" title="Diphthong">
        diphthongs
       </a>
       , ⟨
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        ae, oe, au, eu
       </span>
       ⟩, and the rare diphthong ⟨
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        ui
       </span>
       ⟩. Finally, there were also long and short ⟨
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        y
       </span>
       ⟩, representing
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /y/
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /yː/
       </span>
       in Greek borrowings, which, however, probably came to be pronounced
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /i/
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /iː/
       </span>
       even before Romance vowel changes started.
      </p>
      <p>
       At least since the 1st century AD, short vowels (except
       <i>
        a
       </i>
       ) differed by quality as well as by length from their long counterparts, the short vowels being lower.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
        <a href="#cite_note-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrandgentMoll199111_19-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrandgentMoll199111-19">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Thus the vowel inventory is usually reconstructed as
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /a – aː/
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /ɛ – eː/
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /ɪ – iː/
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /ɔ – oː/
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /ʊ – uː/
       </span>
       .
      </p>
      <table align="right" class="wikitable">

          /u/
         </span>
        </td>
       </tr>
