      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Monophthongization">
        Monophthongization
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Vulgar_Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Monophthongization">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       Many diphthongs had begun their
       <a href="/wiki/Monophthong" title="Monophthong">
        monophthongization
       </a>
       very early. It is presumed that by Republican times,
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        ae
       </span>
       had become
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /ɛː/
       </span>
       in unstressed syllables, a phenomenon that would spread to stressed positions around the 1st century AD.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPalmer1954157_20-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPalmer1954157-20">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
       From the 2nd century AD, there are instances of spellings with ⟨
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        ĕ
       </span>
       ⟩ instead of ⟨
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        ae
       </span>
       ⟩.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrandgentMoll1991118_21-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrandgentMoll1991118-21">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
       ⟨oe⟩ was always a rare diphthong in Classical Latin (in
       <a href="/wiki/Old_Latin" title="Old Latin">
        Old Latin
       </a>
       ,
       <i>
        oinos
       </i>
       regularly became
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        unus
       </span>
       ("one")) and became
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /eː/
       </span>
       during early Imperial times. Thus, one can find
       <i>
        penam
       </i>
       for
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        poenam
       </span>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPalmer1954157_20-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPalmer1954157-20">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       However, ⟨
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        au
       </span>
       ⟩ lasted much longer. While it was monophthongized to
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /o/
       </span>
       in areas of north and central Italy (including Rome), it was retained in most Vulgar Latin, and it survives in modern Romanian (for example,
       <i>
        aur
       </i>
       &lt;
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        aurum
       </span>
       ). There is evidence in French and Spanish that the monophthongization of
       <i>
        au
       </i>
       occurred independently in those languages.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPalmer1954157_20-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPalmer1954157-20">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Loss_of_distinctive_length_and_near-close_mergers">
        Loss of distinctive length and near-close mergers
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Vulgar_Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Loss of distinctive length and near-close mergers">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       Length confusions seem to have begun in unstressed vowels, but they were soon generalized.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerman200028-29_22-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerman200028-29-22">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In the 3rd century AD,
       <a href="/wiki/Marius_Plotius_Sacerdos" title="Marius Plotius Sacerdos">
        Sacerdos
       </a>
       mentions people's tendency to shorten vowels at the end of a word, while some poets (like
       <a href="/wiki/Commodian" title="Commodian">
        Commodian
       </a>
       ) show inconsistencies between long and short vowels in versification.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerman200028-29_22-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerman200028-29-22">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, the loss of contrastive length caused only the merger of
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        ă
       </span>
       and
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        ā
       </span>
       while the rest of pairs remained distinct in quality:
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /a/
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /ɛ – e/
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /ɪ – i/
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /ɔ – o/
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /ʊ – u/
       </span>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPalmer1954156_23-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPalmer1954156-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Also, the near-close vowels
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /ɪ/
       </span>
       and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /ʊ/
       </span>
       became more open in most varieties and merged with
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /e/
       </span>
       and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /o/
       </span>
       respectively.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPalmer1954156_23-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPalmer1954156-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       As a result, the reflexes of Latin
       <i>
        pira
       </i>
       "pear" and
       <i>
        vēra
       </i>
       "true" rhyme in most Romance languages: Italian and Spanish
       <i>
        pera
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        vera
       </i>
       . Similarly, Latin
       <i>
        nucem
       </i>
       "walnut" and
       <i>
        vōcem
       </i>
       "voice" become Italian
       <i>
        noce, voce
       </i>
       , Portuguese
       <i>
        noz, voz
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       There was likely some regional variation in pronunciation, as the Romanian languages and
       <a href="/wiki/Sardinian_language" title="Sardinian language">
        Sardinian
       </a>
       evolved differently.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vincent_24-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Vincent-24">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In Sardinian, all corresponding short and long vowels simply merged with each other, creating a 5-vowel system:
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /a, e, i, o, u/
       </span>
       . In
       <a href="/wiki/Romanian_language" title="Romanian language">
        Romanian
       </a>
       , the front vowels
       <i>
        ĕ, ĭ, ē, ī
       </i>
       evolved like the Western languages, but the back vowels
       <i>
        ŏ, ŭ, ō, ū
       </i>
       evolved as in Sardinian. A few Southern Italian languages, such as southern
       <a href="/wiki/Corsican_language" title="Corsican language">
        Corsican
       </a>
       , northernmost
       <a href="/wiki/Calabria" title="Calabria">
        Calabrian
       </a>
       and southern
       <a href="/wiki/Lucania" title="Lucania">
        Lucanian
       </a>
       , behave like Sardinian with its penta-vowel system or, in case of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vegliote" title="Vegliote">
        Vegliote
       </a>
       (even if only partially) and western
       <a href="/wiki/Lucania" title="Lucania">
        Lucanian
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
        <a href="#cite_note-25">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
       like Romanian.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Phonologization_of_stress">
        Phonologization of stress
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Vulgar_Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Phonologization of stress">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       The placement of stress did not change from Classical to Vulgar Latin, and words continued to be stressed on the same syllable they were before. However, the loss of distinctive length disrupted the correlation between syllable weight and stress placement that existed in Classical Latin. Where in Classical Latin the place of the accent was predictable from the structure of the word, it was no longer so in Vulgar Latin. Stress had become a phonological property and could serve to distinguish forms that were otherwise homophones.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Lengthening_of_stressed_open_syllables">
        Lengthening of stressed open syllables
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Vulgar_Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Lengthening of stressed open syllables">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       After the Classical Latin
       <a href="/wiki/Vowel_length" title="Vowel length">
        vowel length
       </a>
       distinctions were lost in favor of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vowel_quality" title="Vowel quality">
        vowel quality
       </a>
       , a new system of
       <a href="/wiki/Allophone" title="Allophone">
        allophonic
       </a>
       vowel quantity appeared sometime between the 4th and 5th centuries. Around then, stressed vowels in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Open_syllable" title="Open syllable">
        open syllables
       </a>
       came to be pronounced long (but still keeping height contrasts), and all the rest became short. For example, long
       <i>
        venis
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /*ˈvɛː.nis/
       </span>
       ,
       <i>
        fori
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /*fɔː.ri/
       </span>
       ,
       <i>
        cathedra
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /*ˈkaː.te.dra/
       </span>
       ; but short
       <i>
        vendo
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /*ˈven.do/
       </span>
       ,
       <i>
        formas
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /*ˈfor.mas/
       </span>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrandgentMoll1991125_26-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrandgentMoll1991125-26">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
       (This allophonic length distinction persists to this day in
       <a href="/wiki/Italian_phonology" title="Italian phonology">
        Italian
       </a>
       .) However, in some regions of Iberia and Gaul, all stressed vowels came to be pronounced long: for example,
       <i>
        porta
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /*ˈpɔːr.ta/
       </span>
       ,
       <i>
        tempus
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /*ˈtɛːm.pus/
       </span>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrandgentMoll1991125_26-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrandgentMoll1991125-26">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In many descendents, several of the long vowels underwent some form of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diphthongization" title="Diphthongization">
        diphthongization
       </a>
       , most extensively in
       <a href="/wiki/Old_French" title="Old French">
        Old French
       </a>
       where five of the seven long vowels were affected by breaking.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Grammar">
        Grammar
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Vulgar_Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Grammar">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Romance_articles">
        Romance articles
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Vulgar_Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Romance articles">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       It is difficult to place the point in which the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Definite_article" title="Definite article">
        definite article
       </a>
       , absent in Latin but present in all Romance languages, arose, largely because the highly colloquial speech in which it arose was seldom written down until the daughter languages had strongly diverged; most surviving texts in early Romance show the articles fully developed.
      </p>
      <p>
       Definite articles evolved from demonstrative
       <a href="/wiki/Pronoun" title="Pronoun">
        pronouns
       </a>
       or
       <a href="/wiki/Adjective" title="Adjective">
        adjectives
       </a>
       (an analogous development is found in many Indo-European languages, including
       <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">
        Greek
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Celtic_languages" title="Celtic languages">
        Celtic
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Germanic_languages" title="Germanic languages">
        Germanic
       </a>
       ); compare the fate of the Latin
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Demonstrative_adjective" title="Demonstrative adjective">
        demonstrative adjective
       </a>
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/ille#Latin" title="wikt:ille">
         ille
        </a>
        ,
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/illa#Latin" title="wikt:illa">
         illa
        </a>
        ,
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/illud#Latin" title="wikt:illud">
         illud
        </a>
       </i>
       "that", in the
       <a href="/wiki/Romance_languages" title="Romance languages">
        Romance languages
       </a>
       , becoming French
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/le#French" title="wikt:le">
         le
        </a>
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/la#French" title="wikt:la">
         la
        </a>
       </i>
       (Old French
       <i>
        li
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        lo
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        la
       </i>
       ), Catalan and Spanish
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/el#Spanish" title="wikt:el">
         el
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/la#Spanish" title="wikt:la">
         la
        </a>
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/lo#Spanish" title="wikt:lo">
         lo
        </a>
       </i>
       , Portuguese
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/o#Portuguese" title="wikt:o">
         o
        </a>
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/a#Portuguese" title="wikt:a">
         a
        </a>
       </i>
       (elision of -l- is a common feature of Portuguese), and Italian
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/il#Italian" title="wikt:il">
         il
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/lo#Italian" title="wikt:lo">
         lo
        </a>
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/la#Italian" title="wikt:la">
         la
        </a>
       </i>
       . Sardinian went its own way here also, forming its article from
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/ipse#Latin" title="wikt:ipse">
         ipse
        </a>
        ,
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/ipsa#Latin" title="wikt:ipsa">
         ipsa
        </a>
       </i>
       "this" (
       <i>
        su, sa
       </i>
       ); some Catalan and Occitan dialects have articles from the same source. While most of the Romance languages put the article before the noun, Romanian has its own way, by putting the article after the noun, e.g.
       <i>
        lupul
       </i>
       ("the wolf" – from *
       <i>
        lupum illum
       </i>
       ) and
       <i>
        omul
       </i>
       ("the man" –
       <i>
        *homo illum
       </i>
       ),
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vincent_24-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Vincent-24">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
       possibly a result of its membership in the
       <a href="/wiki/Balkan_sprachbund" title="Balkan sprachbund">
        Balkan sprachbund
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       This demonstrative is used in a number of contexts in some early texts in ways that suggest that the Latin demonstrative was losing its force. The
       <a href="/wiki/Vetus_Latina" title="Vetus Latina">
        Vetus Latina
       </a>
       Bible contains a passage
       <i>
        Est tamen ille daemon sodalis peccati
       </i>
       ("The devil is a companion of sin"), in a context that suggests that the word meant little more than an article. The need to translate
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sacred_text" title="Sacred text">
        sacred texts
       </a>
       that were originally in
       <a href="/wiki/Koine_Greek" title="Koine Greek">
        Koine Greek
       </a>
       , which had a definite article, may have given Christian Latin an incentive to choose a substitute.
       <a href="/wiki/Egeria_(pilgrim)" title="Egeria (pilgrim)">
        Aetheria
       </a>
       uses
       <i>
        ipse
       </i>
       similarly:
       <i>
        per mediam vallem ipsam
       </i>
       ("through the middle of the valley"), suggesting that it too was weakening in force.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harrington_14-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Harrington-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Another indication of the weakening of the demonstratives can be inferred from the fact that at this time, legal and similar texts begin to swarm with
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/praedictus#Latin" title="wikt:praedictus">
         praedictus
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/supradictus#Latin" title="wikt:supradictus">
         supradictus
        </a>
       </i>
       , and so forth (all meaning, essentially, "aforesaid"), which seem to mean little more than "this" or "that". Gregory of Tours writes,
       <i>
        Erat autem... beatissimus Anianus in supradicta civitate episcopus
       </i>
       ("Blessed Anianus was bishop in that city.") The original Latin demonstrative adjectives were no longer felt to be strong or specific enough.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harrington_14-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Harrington-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In less formal speech, reconstructed forms suggest that the inherited Latin demonstratives were made more forceful by being compounded with
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/ecce#Latin" title="wikt:ecce">
         ecce
        </a>
       </i>
       (originally an
       <a href="/wiki/Interjection" title="Interjection">
        interjection
       </a>
       : "behold!"), which also spawned Italian
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/ecco#Italian" title="wikt:ecco">
         ecco
        </a>
       </i>
       through
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/eccum#Latin" title="wikt:eccum">
         eccum
        </a>
       </i>
       , a contracted form of
       <i>
        ecce eum
       </i>
       . This is the origin of Old French
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/cil#Old_French" title="wikt:cil">
         cil
        </a>
       </i>
       (*
       <i>
        ecce ille
       </i>
       ),
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/cist#Old_French" title="wikt:cist">
         cist
        </a>
       </i>
       (*
       <i>
        ecce iste
       </i>
       ) and
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/ici#Old_French" title="wikt:ici">
         ici
        </a>
       </i>
       (*
       <i>
        ecce hic
       </i>
       ); Italian
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/questo#Italian" title="wikt:questo">
         questo
        </a>
       </i>
       (*
       <i>
        eccum istum
       </i>
       ),
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/quello#Italian" title="wikt:quello">
         quello
        </a>
       </i>
       (*
       <i>
        eccum illum
       </i>
       ) and (now mainly Tuscan)
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/codesto#Italian" title="wikt:codesto">
         codesto
        </a>
       </i>
       (*
       <i>
        eccum tibi istum
       </i>
       ), as well as
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/qui#Italian" title="wikt:qui">
         qui
        </a>
       </i>
       (*
       <i>
        eccu hic
       </i>
       ),
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/qua#Italian" title="wikt:qua">
         qua
        </a>
       </i>
       (*
       <i>
        eccum hac
       </i>
       ); Spanish
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/aquel#Spanish" title="wikt:aquel">
         aquel
        </a>
       </i>
       and Portuguese
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/aquele#Portuguese" title="wikt:aquele">
         aquele
        </a>
       </i>
       (*
       <i>
        eccum ille
       </i>
       ); Spanish
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/ac%C3%A1#Spanish" title="wikt:acá">
         acá
        </a>
       </i>
       and Portuguese
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/c%C3%A1#Portuguese" title="wikt:cá">
         cá
        </a>
       </i>
       (*
       <i>
        eccum hac
       </i>
       ); Spanish
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/aqu%C3%AD#Spanish" title="wikt:aquí">
         aquí
        </a>
       </i>
       and Portuguese
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/aqui#Portuguese" title="wikt:aqui">
         aqui
        </a>
       </i>
       (*
       <i>
        eccum hic
       </i>
       ); Portuguese
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/acol%C3%A1#Portuguese" title="wikt:acolá">
         acolá
        </a>
       </i>
       (*
       <i>
        eccum illac
       </i>
       ) and
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/aqu%C3%A9m#Portuguese" title="wikt:aquém">
         aquém
        </a>
       </i>
       (*
       <i>
        eccum inde
       </i>
       ); Romanian
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/acest#Romanian" title="wikt:acest">
         acest
        </a>
       </i>
       (*
       <i>
        ecce iste
       </i>
       ) and
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/acela#Romanian" title="wikt:acela">
         acela
        </a>
       </i>
       (*
       <i>
        ecce ille
       </i>
       ), and many other forms.
      </p>
      <p>
       On the other hand, even in the
       <a href="/wiki/Oaths_of_Strasbourg" title="Oaths of Strasbourg">
        Oaths of Strasbourg
       </a>
       , no demonstrative appears even in places where one would clearly be called for in all the later languages (
       <i>
        pro christian poblo
       </i>
       – "for the Christian people"). Using the demonstratives as articles may have still been considered overly informal for a royal oath in the 9th century. Considerable variation exists in all of the Romance vernaculars as to their actual use: in Romanian, the articles are suffixed to the noun (or an adjective preceding it), as in other members of the
       <a href="/wiki/Balkan_sprachbund" title="Balkan sprachbund">
        Balkan sprachbund
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/North_Germanic_languages" title="North Germanic languages">
        North Germanic languages
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The numeral
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/unus#Latin" title="wikt:unus">
         unus
        </a>
        , una
       </i>
       (one) supplies the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indefinite_article" title="Indefinite article">
        indefinite article
       </a>
       in all cases (again, this is a common semantic development across Europe). This is anticipated in Classical Latin;
       <a href="/wiki/Cicero" title="Cicero">
        Cicero
       </a>
       writes
       <i>
        cum uno gladiatore nequissimo
       </i>
       ("with a most immoral gladiator"). This suggests that
       <i>
        unus
       </i>
       was beginning to supplant
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/quidam#Latin" title="wikt:quidam">
         quidam
        </a>
       </i>
       in the meaning of "a certain" or "some" by the 1st century BC.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Accuracy_dispute#Disputed_statement" title="Wikipedia:Accuracy dispute">
          <span title="The material near this tag is possibly inaccurate or nonfactual. (May 2009)">
           dubious
          </span>
         </a>
         <span class="metadata">
          –
          <a href="/wiki/Talk:Vulgar_Latin#Dubious" title="Talk:Vulgar Latin">
           discuss
          </a>
         </span>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Loss_of_neuter">
        Loss of neuter
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Vulgar_Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Loss of neuter">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <table align="right" class="wikitable">
       <caption>
        First and second adjectival declension paradigm in Classical Latin. E.g.,
        <i>
         altus
        </i>
        ("tall")
       </caption>
       <tr>
        <th rowspan="2">
        </th>

        </th>

        </th>

        </th>

        </th>

         neuter
        </th>
        <th>
         feminine
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr align="center">
        <th>
         nominative
        </th>
        <td>
         <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/altus#Latin" title="wikt:altus">
          altus
         </a>
        </td>
        <td>
         altum
        </td>
        <td>
         alta
        </td>
        <td>
         altī
        </td>
        <td>
         alta
        </td>
        <td>
         altae
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr align="center">
        <th>
         accusative
        </th>
        <td colspan="2">
         altum
        </td>
        <td>
         altam
        </td>
        <td>
         altōs
        </td>
        <td>
         alta
        </td>
        <td>
         altās
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr align="center">
        <th>
         dative
        </th>
        <td colspan="2">
         altō
        </td>
        <td>
         altae
        </td>
        <td colspan="3">
         altīs
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr align="center">
        <th>
         ablative
        </th>
        <td colspan="2">
         altō
        </td>
        <td>
         altā
        </td>
        <td colspan="3">
         altīs
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr align="center">
        <th>
         genitive
        </th>
        <td colspan="2">
         altī
        </td>

         altae
        </td>
        <td colspan="2">
         altōrum
        </td>
        <td>
         altārum
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:LatinGenderLoss.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="91" data-file-width="415" height="88" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/LatinGenderLoss.svg/400px-LatinGenderLoss.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/LatinGenderLoss.svg/600px-LatinGenderLoss.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/LatinGenderLoss.svg/800px-LatinGenderLoss.svg.png 2x" width="400"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:LatinGenderLoss.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The genders
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The three
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grammatical_genders" title="Grammatical genders">
        grammatical genders
       </a>
       of Classical Latin were replaced by a two-gender system in most Romance languages.
      </p>
      <p>
       The neuter gender of classical Latin was in most cases identical with the masculine both syntactically and morphologically. The confusion starts already in
       <a href="/wiki/Pompeii" title="Pompeii">
        Pompeian
       </a>
       graffiti, e.g.,
       <i>
        cadaver mortuus
       </i>
       for
       <i>
        cadaver mortuum
       </i>
       ("dead body"), and
       <i>
        hoc locum
       </i>
       for
       <i>
        hunc locum
       </i>
       ("this place"). The morphological confusion shows primarily in the adoption of the nominative ending
       <i>
        -us
       </i>
       (
       <i>
        -Ø
       </i>
       after
       <i>
        -r
       </i>
       ) in the
       <i>
        o
       </i>
       -declension.
      </p>
      <p>
       In
       <a href="/wiki/Petronius" title="Petronius">
        Petronius
       </a>
       ' work, one can find
       <i>
        balneus
       </i>
       for
       <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/balneum#Latin" title="wikt:balneum">
        balneum
       </a>
       ("bath"),
       <i>
        fatus
       </i>
       for
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/fatum#Latin" title="wikt:fatum">
         fatum
        </a>
       </i>
       ("fate"),
       <i>
        caelus
       </i>
       for
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/caelum#Latin" title="wikt:caelum">
         caelum
        </a>
       </i>
       ("heaven"),
       <i>
        amphitheater
       </i>
       for
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/amphitheatrum#Latin" title="wikt:amphitheatrum">
         amphitheatrum
        </a>
       </i>
       ("amphitheatre"),
       <i>
        vinus
       </i>
       for
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/vinum#Latin" title="wikt:vinum">
         vinum
        </a>
       </i>
       ("wine"), and conversely,
       <i>
        thesaurum
       </i>
       for
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/thesaurus#Latin" title="wikt:thesaurus">
         thesaurus
        </a>
       </i>
       ("treasure"). Most of these forms occur in the speech of one man: Trimalchion, an uneducated, Greek (i.e., foreign)
       <a href="/wiki/Freedman" title="Freedman">
        freedman
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       In modern Romance languages, the nominative
       <i>
        s
       </i>
       -ending has been largely abandoned, and all substantives of the
       <i>
        o
       </i>
       -declension have an ending derived from
       <i>
        -um
       </i>
       :
       <i>
        -u
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        -o
       </i>
       , or
       <i>
        -Ø
       </i>
       . E.g., masculine
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/murum#Latin" title="wikt:murum">
         murum
        </a>
       </i>
       ("wall"), and neuter
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/caelum#Latin" title="wikt:caelum">
         caelum
        </a>
       </i>
       ("sky") have evolved to: Italian
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/muro#Italian" title="wikt:muro">
         muro
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/cielo#Italian" title="wikt:cielo">
         cielo
        </a>
       </i>
       ; Portuguese
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/muro#Portuguese" title="wikt:muro">
         muro
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/c%C3%A9u#Portuguese" title="wikt:céu">
         céu
        </a>
       </i>
       ; Spanish
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/muro#Spanish" title="wikt:muro">
         muro
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/cielo#Spanish" title="wikt:cielo">
         cielo
        </a>
       </i>
       , Catalan
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/mur#Catalan" title="wikt:mur">
         mur
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/cel#Catalan" title="wikt:cel">
         cel
        </a>
       </i>
       ; Romanian
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/mur#Romanian" title="wikt:mur">
         mur
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        cieru&gt;
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/cer#Romanian" title="wikt:cer">
         cer
        </a>
       </i>
       ; French
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/mur#French" title="wikt:mur">
         mur
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/ciel#French" title="wikt:ciel">
         ciel
        </a>
       </i>
       . However, Old French still had
       <i>
        -s
       </i>
       in the nominative and
       <i>
        -Ø
       </i>
       in the accusative in both words:
       <i>
        murs
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        ciels
       </i>
       [nominative] –
       <i>
        mur
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        ciel
       </i>
       [oblique].
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">
        <a href="#cite_note-27">
         [27]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       For some neuter nouns of the third declension, the oblique stem became the productive; for others, the nominative/accusative form, which was identical in Classical Latin. Evidence suggests that the neuter gender was under pressure well back into the imperial period. French
       <i>
        (le)
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/lait#French" title="wikt:lait">
         lait
        </a>
       </i>
       , Catalan
       <i>
        (la)
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/llet#Catalan" title="wikt:llet">
         llet
        </a>
       </i>
       , Spanish
       <i>
        (la)
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/leche#Spanish" title="wikt:leche">
         leche
        </a>
       </i>
       , Portuguese
       <i>
        (o)
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/leite#Portuguese" title="wikt:leite">
         leite
        </a>
       </i>
       , Italian language
       <i>
        (il)
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/latte#Italian" title="wikt:latte">
         latte
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Leonese_language" title="Leonese language">
        Leonese
       </a>
       <i>
        (el) lleche
       </i>
       and Romanian
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/lapte#Romanian" title="wikt:lapte">
         lapte
        </a>
        (le)
       </i>
       ("milk"), all derive from the non-standard but attested Latin nominative/accusative neuter
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/lacte#Latin" title="wikt:lacte">
         lacte
        </a>
       </i>
       or accusative masculine
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/lactem#Latin" title="wikt:lactem">
         lactem
        </a>
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Note also that in Spanish the word became feminine, while in French, Portuguese and Italian it became masculine (in Romanian it remained neuter,
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/lapte#Romanian" title="wikt:lapte">
         lapte
        </a>
        /lăpturi
       </i>
       ). Other neuter forms, however, were preserved in Romance; Catalan and French
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/nom#French" title="wikt:nom">
         nom
        </a>
       </i>
       , Leonese, Portuguese and Italian
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/nome#Italian" title="wikt:nome">
         nome
        </a>
       </i>
       , Romanian
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/nume#Romanian" title="wikt:nume">
         nume
        </a>
       </i>
       ("name") all preserve the Latin nominative/accusative
       <i>
        nomen
       </i>
       , rather than the oblique stem form *
       <i>
        nominem
       </i>
       (which nevertheless produced Spanish
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/nombre#Spanish" title="wikt:nombre">
         nombre
        </a>
       </i>
       ).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vincent_24-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Vincent-24">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <table align="right" border="0" class="wikitable" style="margin: 10px;">
       <caption>
        Typical Italian endings
       </caption>
       <tr>
        <th>
        </th>

        </th>

        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr align="center">
        <th>

        </th>

        </th>

        </th>

        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr align="center">
        <th>
         masculine
        </th>
        <td>
         <i>
          <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/giardino#Italian" title="wikt:giardino">
           giardin
           <u>
            o
           </u>
          </a>
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          giardin
          <u>
           i
          </u>
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/buono#Italian" title="wikt:buono">
           buon
           <u>
            o
           </u>
          </a>
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          buon
          <u>
           i
          </u>
         </i>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr align="center">
        <th>
         feminine
        </th>
        <td>
         <i>
          <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/donna#Italian" title="wikt:donna">
           donn
           <u>
            a
           </u>
          </a>
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          donn
          <u>
           e
          </u>
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/buona#Italian" title="wikt:buona">
           buon
           <u>

           </u>
          </a>
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          buon
          <u>
           e
          </u>
         </i>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr align="center">
        <th>
         neuter
        </th>
        <td>
         <i>
          <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/uovo#Italian" title="wikt:uovo">
           uov
           <u>
            o
           </u>
          </a>
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          uov
          <u>
           a
          </u>
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/buono#Italian" title="wikt:buono">
           buon
           <u>
            o
           </u>
          </a>
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          buon
          <u>
           e
          </u>
         </i>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       Most neuter nouns had plural forms ending in
       <small>
        -A
       </small>
       or
       <small>
        -IA
       </small>
       ; some of these were reanalysed as feminine singulars, such as
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/gaudium#Latin" title="wikt:gaudium">
         gaudium
        </a>
       </i>
       ("joy"), plural
       <i>
        gaudia
       </i>
       ; the plural form lies at the root of the French feminine singular
       <i>
        (la)
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/joie#French" title="wikt:joie">
         joie
        </a>
       </i>
       , as well as of Catalan and Occitan
       <i>
        (la)
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/joia#Occitan" title="wikt:joia">
         joia
        </a>
       </i>
       (Italian
       <i>
        la
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/gioia#Italian" title="wikt:gioia">
         gioia
        </a>
       </i>
       is a borrowing from French); the same for
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/lignum#Latin" title="wikt:lignum">
         lignum
        </a>
       </i>
       ("wood stick"), plural
       <i>
        ligna
       </i>
       , that originated the Catalan feminine singular noun
       <i>
        (la)
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/llenya#Catalan" title="wikt:llenya">
         llenya
        </a>
       </i>
       , and Spanish
       <i>
        (la)
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/le%C3%B1a#Spanish" title="wikt:leña">
         leña
        </a>
       </i>
       . Some Romance languages still have a special form derived from the ancient neuter plural which is treated grammatically as feminine: e.g.,
       <small>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/bracchium#Latin" title="wikt:bracchium">
         BRACCHIUM
        </a>
        : BRACCHIA
       </small>
       "arm(s)" → Italian
       <i>
        (il)
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/braccio#Italian" title="wikt:braccio">
         braccio
        </a>
       </i>
       :
       <i>
        (le) braccia
       </i>
       , Romanian
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/bra%C8%9B#Romanian" title="wikt:braț">
         braț(ul)
        </a>
       </i>
       :
       <i>
        brațe(le)
       </i>
       . Cf. also
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Merovingian" title="Merovingian">
        Merovingian
       </a>
       Latin
       <i>
        ipsa animalia aliquas mortas fuerant
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Alternations in Italian
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heteroclitic" title="Heteroclitic">
        heteroclitic
       </a>
       nouns such as
       <i>
        l'uovo fresco
       </i>
       ("the fresh egg") /
       <i>
        le uova fresche
       </i>
       ("the fresh eggs") are usually analysed as masculine in the singular and feminine in the plural, with an irregular plural in
       <i>
        -a
       </i>
       . However, it is also consistent with their historical development to say that
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/uovo#Italian" title="wikt:uovo">
         uovo
        </a>
       </i>
       is simply a regular neuter noun (
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/ovum#Latin" title="wikt:ovum">
         ovum
        </a>
       </i>
       , plural
       <i>
        ova
       </i>
       ) and that the characteristic ending for words agreeing with these nouns is
       <i>
        -o
       </i>
       in the singular and
       <i>
        -e
       </i>
       in the plural. The same alternation in gender exists in certain Romanian nouns, but is considered regular as it is more common than in Italian. Thus, a relict neuter gender can arguably be said to persist in Italian and Romanian.
      </p>
      <p>
       In Portuguese, traces of the neuter plural can be found in collective formations and words meant to inform a bigger size or sturdiness. Thus, one can use
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/ovo#Portuguese" title="wikt:ovo">
         ovo
        </a>
        /ovos
       </i>
       ("egg/eggs") and
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/ova#Portuguese" title="wikt:ova">
         ova
        </a>
        /ovas
       </i>
       ("roe", "a collection of eggs"),
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/bordo#Portuguese" title="wikt:bordo">
         bordo
        </a>
        /bordos
       </i>
       ("section(s) of an edge") and
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/borda#Portuguese" title="wikt:borda">
         borda
        </a>
        /bordas
       </i>
       ("edge/edges"),
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/saco#Portuguese" title="wikt:saco">
         saco
        </a>
        /sacos
       </i>
       ("bag/bags") and
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/saca#Portuguese" title="wikt:saca">
         saca
        </a>
        /sacas
       </i>
       ("sack/sacks"),
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/manto#Portuguese" title="wikt:manto">
         manto
        </a>
        /mantos
       </i>
       ("cloak/cloaks") and
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/manta#Portuguese" title="wikt:manta">
         manta
        </a>
        /mantas
       </i>
       ("blanket/blankets"). Other times, it resulted in words whose gender may be changed more or less arbitrarily, like
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/fruto#Portuguese" title="wikt:fruto">
         fruto
        </a>
        /
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/fruta#Portuguese" title="wikt:fruta">
         fruta
        </a>
       </i>
       ("fruit"),
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/caldo#Portuguese" title="wikt:caldo">
         caldo
        </a>
        /
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/calda#Portuguese" title="wikt:calda">
         calda
        </a>
       </i>
       (broth"), etc.
      </p>
      <p>
       These formations were especially common when they could be used to avoid irregular forms. In Latin, the names of trees were usually feminine, but many were declined in the second declension paradigm, which was dominated by masculine or neuter nouns. Latin
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/pirus#Latin" title="wikt:pirus">
         pirus
        </a>
       </i>
       ("
       <a href="/wiki/Pear" title="Pear">
        pear
       </a>
       tree"), a feminine noun with a masculine-looking ending, became masculine in Italian
       <i>
        (il)
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/pero#Italian" title="wikt:pero">
         pero
        </a>
       </i>
       and Romanian
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/p%C4%83r#Romanian" title="wikt:păr">
         păr(ul)
        </a>
       </i>
       ; in French and Spanish it was replaced by the masculine derivations
       <i>
        (le)
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/poirier#French" title="wikt:poirier">
         poirier
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        (el)
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/peral#Spanish" title="wikt:peral">
         peral
        </a>
       </i>
       ; and in Portuguese and Catalan by the feminine derivations
       <i>
        (a)
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/pereira#Portuguese" title="wikt:pereira">
         pereira
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        (la)
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/perera#Catalan" title="wikt:perera">
         perera
        </a>
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       As usual, irregularities persisted longest in frequently used forms. From the fourth declension noun
       <i>
        manus
       </i>
       ("hand"), another feminine noun with the ending
       <i>
        -us
       </i>
       , Italian and Spanish derived
       <i>
        (la)
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/mano#Spanish" title="wikt:mano">
         mano
        </a>
       </i>
       , Romanian
       <i>
        mânu&gt;
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/m%C3%A2na#Romanian" title="wikt:mâna">
         mâna
        </a>
       </i>
       pl (reg.)
       <i>
        mânule/
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/m%C3%A2nuri#Romanian" title="wikt:mânuri">
         mânuri
        </a>
       </i>
       , Catalan
       <i>
        (la)
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/m%C3%A0#Catalan" title="wikt:mà">
         mà
        </a>
       </i>
       , and Portuguese
       <i>
        (a)
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/m%C3%A3o#Portuguese" title="wikt:mão">
         mão
        </a>
       </i>
       , which preserve the feminine gender along with the masculine appearance.
      </p>
      <p>
       Except for the Italian and Romanian heteroclitic nouns, other major Romance languages have no trace of neuter nouns, but still have neuter pronouns. French
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/celui-ci#French" title="wikt:celui-ci">
         celui-ci
        </a>
        /
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/celle-ci#French" title="wikt:celle-ci">
         celle-ci
        </a>
        /
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/ceci#French" title="wikt:ceci">
         ceci
        </a>
       </i>
       ("this"), Spanish
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%C3%A9ste#Spanish" title="wikt:éste">
         éste
        </a>
        /
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%C3%A9sta#Spanish" title="wikt:ésta">
         ésta
        </a>
        /
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/esto#Spanish" title="wikt:esto">
         esto
        </a>
       </i>
       ("this"), Italian:
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/gli#Italian" title="wikt:gli">
         gli
        </a>
        /
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/le#Italian" title="wikt:le">
         le
        </a>
        /
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/ci#Italian" title="wikt:ci">
         ci
        </a>
       </i>
       ("to him" /"to her" / "to it"), Catalan:
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/ho#Catalan" title="wikt:ho">
         ho
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/a%C3%A7%C3%B2#Catalan" title="wikt:açò">
         açò
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/aix%C3%B2#Catalan" title="wikt:això">
         això
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/all%C3%B2#Catalan" title="wikt:allò">
         allò
        </a>
       </i>
       ("it" /
       <i>
        this
       </i>
       /
       <i>
        this-that
       </i>
       /
       <i>
        that over there
       </i>
       ); Portuguese:
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/todo#Portuguese" title="wikt:todo">
         todo
        </a>
        /
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/toda#Portuguese" title="wikt:toda">
         toda
        </a>
        /
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/tudo#Portuguese" title="wikt:tudo">
         tudo
        </a>
       </i>
       ("all of him" / "all of her" / "all of it").
      </p>
      <p>
       In Spanish, a three-way contrast is also made with the definite articles
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/el#Spanish" title="wikt:el">
         el
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/la#Spanish" title="wikt:la">
         la
        </a>
       </i>
       , and
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/lo#Spanish" title="wikt:lo">
         lo
        </a>
       </i>
       . The last is used with nouns denoting abstract categories:
       <i>
        lo bueno
       </i>
       , literally "that which is good", from
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/bueno#Spanish" title="wikt:bueno">
         bueno
        </a>
       </i>
       : good.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Loss_of_oblique_cases">
        Loss of oblique cases
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Vulgar_Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Loss of oblique cases">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The Vulgar Latin vowel shifts caused the merger of several case endings in the nominal and adjectival declensions.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerman200052_28-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerman200052-28">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Some of the causes include: the loss of final
       <i>
        m
       </i>
       , the merger of
       <i>
        ă
       </i>
       with
       <i>
        ā
       </i>
       , and the merger of
       <i>
        ŭ
       </i>
       with
       <i>
        ō
       </i>
       (see tables).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerman200052_28-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerman200052-28">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Thus, by the 5th century, the number of case contrasts had been drastically reduced.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerman200052_28-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerman200052-28">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="div-col columns column-count column-count-2" style="-moz-column-count: 2; -webkit-column-count: 2; column-count: 2;">
       <table align="center" class="wikitable">
        <caption>
         Evolution of a 1st declension noun:
         <br/>

         </i>
         ("onion") (feminine singular)
        </caption>

         <th>

          </sup>

         </th>

         </th>
        </tr>

         </th>

         </th>
        </tr>
       </table>
       <table align="center" class="wikitable">

           </a>

         </th>

         </th>
        </tr>

        </tr>

          accusative
         </th>

          </i>
         </td>
         <td>
          <i>
           *múru
          </i>
         </td>
         <td rowspan="4">
          <i>
           mur
          </i>
         </td>
        </tr>
        <tr align="center">
         <th>
          ablative
         </th>
         <td rowspan="2">
          <i>
           mūrō
          </i>
         </td>
         <td rowspan="2">
          <i>
           *múro
          </i>
         </td>
        </tr>
        <tr align="center">
         <th>
          dative
         </th>
        </tr>
        <tr align="center">
         <th>
          genitive
         </th>
         <td>
          <i>
           mūrī
          </i>
         </td>
         <td>
          <i>
           *múri
          </i>
         </td>
        </tr>
       </table>
      </div>
      <p>
       There also seems to be a marked tendency to confuse different forms even when they have not become homophonous (like in the generally more distinct plurals), which indicates nominal declension was not only shaped by phonetic mergers, but also by structural factors.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerman200052_28-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerman200052-28">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
       As a result of the untenability of the noun case system after these phonetic changes, Vulgar Latin shifted from a markedly
       <a href="/wiki/Synthetic_language" title="Synthetic language">
        synthetic language
       </a>
       to a more
       <a href="/wiki/Analytic_language" title="Analytic language">
        analytic one
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <b>
        <a href="/wiki/Genitive_case" title="Genitive case">
         genitive case
        </a>
       </b>
       died out around the 3rd century AD, according to
       <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_Meyer-L%C3%BCbke" title="Wilhelm Meyer-Lübke">
        Meyer-Lübke
       </a>
       , and began to be replaced by
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        de
       </span>
       + noun as early as the 2nd century BC
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2016)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       . Exceptions of remaining genitive forms are some pronouns, many fossilized combinations like sayings, some proper names, and certain terms related to the church. For example, French
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/jeudi#French" title="wikt:jeudi">
         jeudi
        </a>
       </i>
       ("Thursday") &lt; Old French
       <i>
        juesdi
       </i>
       &lt; Vulgar Latin
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/dies_Iovis#Latin" title="wikt:dies Iovis">
         jovis diēs
        </a>
       </span>
       ; Spanish
       <i>
        es
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/menester#Spanish" title="wikt:menester">
         menester
        </a>
       </i>
       ("it is necessary") &lt;
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        est
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/ministeri#Latin" title="wikt:ministeri">
         ministeri
        </a>
       </span>
       ; terms like
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        angelorum
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        paganorum
       </span>
       ; and Italian
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/terremoto#Italian" title="wikt:terremoto">
         terremoto
        </a>
       </i>
       ("earthquake") &lt;
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/terraemotus#Latin" title="wikt:terraemotus">
         terrae motu
        </a>
       </span>
       as well as names like
       <i>
        Paoli
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        Pieri
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrandgentMoll199182_29-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrandgentMoll199182-29">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <b>
        <a href="/wiki/Dative_case" title="Dative case">
         dative case
        </a>
       </b>
       lasted longer than the genitive, even though
       <a href="/wiki/Plautus" title="Plautus">
        Plautus
       </a>
       , in the 2nd century BC, already shows some instances of substitution by the construction
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        ad
       </span>
       + accusative. For example,
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        ad carnuficem dabo
       </span>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrandgentMoll199182_29-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrandgentMoll199182-29">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
        <a href="#cite_note-30">
         [30]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <b>
        <a href="/wiki/Accusative_case" title="Accusative case">
         accusative case
        </a>
       </b>
       developed as a prepositional case, displacing many instances of the
       <a href="/wiki/Ablative_case" title="Ablative case">
        ablative
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrandgentMoll199182_29-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrandgentMoll199182-29">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Towards the end of the imperial period, the accusative came to be used more and more as a general oblique case.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerman200053_31-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerman200053-31">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Despite increasing case mergers, nominative and accusative forms seem to have remained distinct for much longer, since they are rarely confused in inscriptions.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerman200053_31-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerman200053-31">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Even though Gaulish texts from the 7th century rarely confuse both forms, it is believed that both cases began to merge in Africa by the end of the empire, and a bit later in parts of Italy and Iberia.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerman200053_31-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerman200053-31">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Nowadays,
       <a href="/wiki/Romanian_language" title="Romanian language">
        Romanian
       </a>
       maintains a two-case system, while
       <a href="/wiki/Old_French" title="Old French">
        Old French
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Old_Occitan" title="Old Occitan">
        Old Occitan
       </a>
       had a two-case subject-oblique system.
      </p>
      <p>
       This Old French system was based largely on whether or not the Latin case ending contained an "s" or not, with the "s" being retained but all vowels in the ending being lost (as with
       <i>
        veisin
       </i>
       below). But since this meant that it was easy to confuse the singular nominative with the plural oblique, and the plural nominative with the singular oblique, along with the final "s" becoming silent, this case system ultimately collapsed as well, and French adopted one case (usually the oblique) for all purposes, leaving the Romanian the only one to survive to the present day.
      </p>
      <table align="center" class="wikitable">
       <caption>

      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Wider_use_of_prepositions">
        Wider use of prepositions
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Vulgar_Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Wider use of prepositions">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Loss of a productive noun case system meant that the
       <a href="/wiki/Syntax" title="Syntax">
        syntactic
       </a>
       purposes it formerly served now had to be performed by
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Preposition" title="Preposition">
        prepositions
       </a>
       and other paraphrases. These particles increased in number, and many new ones were formed by compounding old ones. The descendant Romance languages are full of grammatical particles such as Spanish
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/donde#Spanish" title="wikt:donde">
         donde
        </a>
       </i>
       , "where", from Latin
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/de#Latin" title="wikt:de">
         de
        </a>
       </i>
       +
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/unde#Latin" title="wikt:unde">
         unde
        </a>
       </i>
       , or French
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/d%C3%A8s#French" title="wikt:dès">
         dès
        </a>
       </i>
       , "since", from
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/de#Latin" title="wikt:de">
         de
        </a>
       </i>
       +
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/ex#Latin" title="wikt:ex">
         ex
        </a>
       </i>
       , while the equivalent Spanish and Portuguese
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/desde#Portuguese" title="wikt:desde">
         desde
        </a>
       </i>
       is
       <i>
        de
       </i>
       +
       <i>
        ex
       </i>
       +
       <i>
        de
       </i>
       . Spanish
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/despu%C3%A9s#Spanish" title="wikt:después">
         después
        </a>
       </i>
       and Portuguese
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/depois#Portuguese" title="wikt:depois">
         depois
        </a>
       </i>
       , "after", represent
       <i>
        de
       </i>
       +
       <i>
        ex
       </i>
       +
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/post#Latin" title="wikt:post">
         post
        </a>
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Some of these new compounds appear in literary texts during the late empire; French
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/dehors#French" title="wikt:dehors">
         dehors
        </a>
       </i>
       , Spanish
       <i>
        de
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/fuera#Spanish" title="wikt:fuera">
         fuera
        </a>
       </i>
       and Portuguese
       <i>
        de
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/fora#Portuguese" title="wikt:fora">
         fora
        </a>
       </i>
       ("outside") all represent
       <i>
        de
       </i>
       +
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/foris#Latin" title="wikt:foris">
         foris
        </a>
       </i>
       (Romanian
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/afar%C4%83#Romanian" title="wikt:afară">
         afară
        </a>
       </i>
       –
       <i>
        ad
       </i>
       +
       <i>
        foris
       </i>
       ), and we find
       <a href="/wiki/Jerome" title="Jerome">
        Jerome
       </a>
       writing
       <i>
        stulti, nonne qui fecit, quod de foris est, etiam id, quod de intus est fecit?
       </i>
       (Luke 11.40: "ye fools, did not he, that made which is without, make that which is within also?"). In some cases, compounds were created by combining a large number of particles, such as the Romanian
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/adineauri#Romanian" title="wikt:adineauri">
         adineauri
        </a>
       </i>
       ("just recently") from
       <i>
        ad
       </i>
       +
       <i>
        de
       </i>
       +
       <i>
        in
       </i>
       +
       <i>
        illa
       </i>
       +
       <i>
        hora
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
        <a href="#cite_note-32">
         [32]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       As Latin was losing its case system, prepositions started to move in to fill the void. In colloquial Latin, the preposition
       <i>
        ad
       </i>
       followed by the accusative was sometimes used as a substitute for the dative case.
      </p>
      <p>
       <b>
        Classical Latin:
       </b>
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <i>
         Marcus patrī librum dat.
        </i>
        "Marcus is giving [his] father [a/the] book."
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       <b>
        Vulgar Latin:
       </b>
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <i>
         *Marco da libru a patre.
        </i>
        "Marcus is giving [a/the] book to [his] father."
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       Just as in the disappearing dative case, colloquial Latin sometimes replaced the disappearing genitive case with the preposition
       <i>
        de
       </i>
       followed by the ablative.
      </p>
      <p>
       <b>
        Classical Latin:
       </b>
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <i>
         Marcus mihi librum patris dat.
        </i>
        "Marcus is giving me [his] father's book.
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       <b>
        Vulgar Latin:
       </b>
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <i>
         *Marco mi da libru de patre.
        </i>
        "Marcus is giving me [the] book of [his] father."
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Pronouns">
        Pronouns
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Vulgar_Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Pronouns">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Unlike in the nominal and adjectival inflections, pronouns kept great part of the case distinctions. However, many changes happened. For example, the
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /ɡ/
       </span>
       of
       <i>
        ego
       </i>
       was lost by the end of the empire, and
       <i>
        eo
       </i>
       appears in manuscripts from the 6th century.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Avoid_weasel_words" title="Wikipedia:Avoid weasel words">
          <span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too vague attribution or weasel words. (April 2017)">
           which?
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrandgentMoll1991238_33-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrandgentMoll1991238-33">
         [33]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable">
       <caption>
        Reconstructed pronominal system of Vulgar Latin
